<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">MPEG-1</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<caption style="font-size:130%; padding-bottom:0.15em;">Moving Picture Experts Group Phase 1 (MPEG-1)</caption>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">Filename extension</a></th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><code><tt>.mpg</tt>, <tt>.mpeg</tt>, <tt>.mp1</tt>, <tt>.mp2</tt>, <tt>.mp3</tt>, <tt>.m1v</tt>, <tt>.m1a</tt>, <tt>.m2a</tt>, <tt>.mpa</tt>, <tt>.mpv</tt></code></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">Internet media type</a></th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><code>audio/mpeg, video/mpeg</code></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Developed by</th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">IEC</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Initial release</th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;">created 1988–92</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Type of format</th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;">audio, video, container</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Extended from</th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a>, <a href="/wiki/H.261" title="H.261">H.261</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">Extended to</th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/MPEG-2" title="MPEG-2">MPEG-2</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">Standard</a></th>
<td style="line-height:1.35em;"><a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>/<a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">IEC</a> 11172</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>MPEG-1</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Technical_standard" title="Technical standard">standard</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lossy" title="Lossy">lossy</a> compression of <a href="/wiki/Video" title="Video">video</a> and <a href="/wiki/Audio_frequency" title="Audio frequency">audio</a>. It is designed to compress <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">VHS</a>-quality raw digital video and CD audio down to 1.5 Mbit/s (26:1 and 6:1 compression ratios respectively)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faqs1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faqs1-1">[1]</a></sup> without excessive quality loss, making <a href="/wiki/Video_CD" title="Video CD">video CDs</a>, digital <a href="/wiki/Cable_television" title="Cable television">cable</a>/<a href="/wiki/Satellite_television" title="Satellite television">satellite</a> TV and <a href="/wiki/Digital_audio_broadcasting" title="Digital audio broadcasting">digital audio broadcasting</a> (DAB) possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurihama89_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-kurihama89-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, MPEG-1 has become the most widely compatible lossy audio/video format in the world, and is used in a large number of products and technologies. Perhaps the best-known part of the MPEG-1 standard is the <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">MP3</a> audio format it introduced.</p>
<p>The MPEG-1 standard is published as <b><a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>/<a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">IEC</a> 11172</b> – Information technology—Coding of moving pictures and associated audio for digital storage media at up to about 1.5 Mbit/s.<br/>
The standard consists of the following five <i>Parts</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jtc1-sc29_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-jtc1-sc29-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-part1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg-achievemnts_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg-achievemnts-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg-terms-of-reference_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg-terms-of-reference-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg-standards_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg-standards-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Systems (storage and synchronization of video, audio, and other data together)</li>
<li>Video (compressed video content)</li>
<li>Audio (compressed audio content)</li>
<li>Conformance testing (testing the correctness of implementations of the standard)</li>
<li>Reference software (example software showing how to encode and decode according to the standard)</li>
</ol>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modeled on the successful collaborative approach and the compression technologies developed by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Photographic_Experts_Group" title="Joint Photographic Experts Group">Joint Photographic Experts Group</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCITT" title="CCITT">CCITT</a>'s <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Experts_Group_on_Telephony&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Experts Group on Telephony (page does not exist)">Experts Group on Telephony</a> (creators of the <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a> image compression standard and the <a href="/wiki/H.261" title="H.261">H.261</a> standard for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_conferencing" title="Video conferencing">video conferencing</a> respectively), the <a href="/wiki/Moving_Picture_Experts_Group" title="Moving Picture Experts Group">Moving Picture Experts Group</a> (MPEG) working group was established in January 1988. MPEG was formed to address the need for <a href="/wiki/Technical_standard" title="Technical standard">standard</a> video and audio formats, and to build on H.261 to get better quality through the use of more complex encoding methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-deadlink_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-deadlink-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Development of the MPEG-1 standard began in May 1988. Fourteen video and fourteen audio codec proposals were submitted by individual companies and institutions for evaluation. The codecs were extensively tested for <a href="/wiki/Computational_complexity_theory" title="Computational complexity theory">computational complexity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjective</a> (human perceived) quality, at data rates of 1.5 Mbit/s. This specific bitrate was chosen for transmission over <a href="/wiki/Digital_Signal_1" title="Digital Signal 1">T-1</a>/<a href="/wiki/E-carrier" title="E-carrier">E-1</a> lines and as the approximate data rate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Book_(audio_CD_standard)" title="Red Book (audio CD standard)">audio CDs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opensource_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-opensource-11">[11]</a></sup> The codecs that excelled in this testing were utilized as the basis for the standard and refined further, with additional features and other improvements being incorporated in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-santa_clara90_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-santa_clara90-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>After 20 meetings of the full group in various cities around the world, and 4½ years of development and testing, the final standard (for parts 1–3) was approved in early November 1992 and published a few months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_meetings_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_meetings-13">[13]</a></sup> The reported completion date of the MPEG-1 standard varies greatly: a largely complete draft standard was produced in September 1990, and from that point on, only minor changes were introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup> The draft standard was publicly available for purchase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faq_draft_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faq_draft-14">[14]</a></sup> The standard was finished with the 6 November 1992 meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The Berkeley Plateau Multimedia Research Group developed an MPEG-1 decoder in November 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In July 1990, before the first draft of the MPEG-1 standard had even been written, work began on a second standard, <a href="/wiki/MPEG-2" title="MPEG-2">MPEG-2</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_achievements_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_achievements-17">[17]</a></sup> intended to extend MPEG-1 technology to provide full broadcast-quality video (as per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCIR_601" title="CCIR 601">CCIR 601</a>) at high bitrates (3–15  Mbit/s) and support for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interlaced" title="Interlaced">interlaced</a> video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-london92_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-london92-18">[18]</a></sup> Due in part to the similarity between the two codecs, the MPEG-2 standard includes full backwards compatibility with MPEG-1 video, so any MPEG-2 decoder can play MPEG-1 videos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sydney93_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-sydney93-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Notably, the MPEG-1 standard very strictly defines the <a href="/wiki/Bitstream" title="Bitstream">bitstream</a>, and decoder function, but does not define how MPEG-1 encoding is to be performed, although a reference implementation is provided in <b>ISO/IEC-11172-5</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faqs1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faqs1-1">[1]</a></sup> This means that MPEG-1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coding_efficiency" title="Coding efficiency">coding efficiency</a> can drastically vary depending on the encoder used, and generally means that newer encoders perform significantly better than their predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faqs2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faqs2-20">[20]</a></sup> The first three parts (Systems, Video and Audio) of ISO/IEC 11172 were published in August 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patents">Patents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Patents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All widely known patent searches suggest that, due to its age, MPEG-1 video and Layer I/II audio is no longer covered by any patents and can thus be used without obtaining a licence or paying any fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-video_resolution_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-video_resolution-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snazzizone_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-snazzizone-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The ISO patent database lists one patent for ISO 11172, US 4,472,747, which expired in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The near-complete draft of the MPEG-1 standard was publicly available as ISO CD 11172<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faq_draft_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faq_draft-14">[14]</a></sup> by December 6, 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Neither the July 2008 Kuro5hin article "Patent Status of MPEG-1, H.261 and MPEG-2",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> nor an August 2008 thread on the gstreamer-devel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> mailing list were able to list a single unexpired MPEG-1 video and Layer I/II audio patent. A May 2009 discussion on the whatwg mailing list mentioned US 5,214,678 patent as possibly covering MPEG audio layer II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Filed in 1990 and published in 1993, this patent is now expired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>A full MPEG-1 decoder and encoder, with "Layer 3 audio", cannot be implemented royalty free since there are companies that require patent fees for implementations of <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">MPEG-1 Layer 3 Audio</a> as discussed in the MP3 article.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Most popular <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> for video playback includes MPEG-1 decoding, in addition to any other supported formats.</li>
<li>The popularity of <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">MP3</a> audio has established a massive <a href="/wiki/Installed_base" title="Installed base">installed base</a> of hardware that can play back MPEG-1 Audio (all three layers).</li>
<li>"Virtually all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_audio_players" title="Digital audio players">digital audio devices</a>" can play back MPEG-1 Audio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg1_audio_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg1_audio-39">[39]</a></sup> Many millions have been sold to-date.</li>
<li>Before <a href="/wiki/MPEG-2" title="MPEG-2">MPEG-2</a> became widespread, many digital satellite/cable TV services used MPEG-1 exclusively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faqs2_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faqs2-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li>The widespread popularity of MPEG-2 with broadcasters means MPEG-1 is playable by most digital cable and satellite <a href="/wiki/Set-top_box" title="Set-top box">set-top boxes</a>, and digital disc and tape players, due to backwards compatibility.</li>
<li>MPEG-1 was used for full-screen video on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Book_(CD-interactive_standard)" title="Green Book (CD-interactive standard)">Green Book</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD-i" title="CD-i">CD-i</a>, and on <a href="/wiki/Video_CD" title="Video CD">Video CD</a> (VCD).</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Super_Video_CD" title="Super Video CD">Super Video CD</a> standard, based on VCD, uses MPEG-1 audio exclusively, as well as MPEG-2 video.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/DVD-Video" title="DVD-Video">DVD-Video</a> format uses MPEG-2 video primarily, but MPEG-1 support is explicitly defined in the standard.</li>
<li>The DVD-Video standard originally required MPEG-1 Layer II audio for PAL countries, but was changed to allow AC-3/<a href="/wiki/Dolby_Digital" title="Dolby Digital">Dolby Digital</a>-only discs. MPEG-1 Layer II audio is still allowed on DVDs, although newer extensions to the format, like <a href="/wiki/MPEG_Multichannel" title="MPEG Multichannel">MPEG Multichannel</a>, are rarely supported.</li>
<li>Most DVD players also support Video CD and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MP3_CD" title="MP3 CD">MP3 CD</a> playback, which use MPEG-1.</li>
<li>The international <a href="/wiki/Digital_Video_Broadcasting" title="Digital Video Broadcasting">Digital Video Broadcasting</a> (DVB) standard primarily uses MPEG-1 Layer II audio, and MPEG-2 video.</li>
<li>The international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_Audio_Broadcasting" title="Digital Audio Broadcasting">Digital Audio Broadcasting</a> (DAB) standard uses MPEG-1 Layer II audio exclusively, due to MP2's especially high quality, modest decoder performance requirements, and tolerance of errors.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_1:_Systems">Part 1: Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Part 1: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Part 1 of the MPEG-1 standard covers <i>systems</i>, and is defined in <b>ISO/IEC-11172-1</b>.</p>
<p>MPEG-1 Systems specifies the logical layout and methods used to store the encoded audio, video, and other data into a standard bitstream, and to maintain synchronization between the different contents. This <a href="/wiki/File_format" title="File format">file format</a> is specifically designed for storage on media, and transmission over <a href="/wiki/Channel_(communications)" title="Channel (communications)">data channels</a>, that are considered relatively reliable. Only limited error protection is defined by the standard, and small errors in the bitstream may cause noticeable defects.</p>
<p>This structure was later named an <a href="/wiki/MPEG_program_stream" title="MPEG program stream">MPEG program stream</a>: "The MPEG-1 Systems design is essentially identical to the MPEG-2 Program Stream structure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg1_systems_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg1_systems-40">[40]</a></sup> This terminology is more popular, precise (differentiates it from an <a href="/wiki/MPEG_transport_stream" title="MPEG transport stream">MPEG transport stream</a>) and will be used here.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elementary_streams">Elementary streams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Elementary streams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Elementary Streams</b> (<b>ES</b>) are the raw bitstreams of MPEG-1 audio and video encoded data (output from an encoder). These files can be distributed on their own, such as is the case with MP3 files.</p>
<p><b>Packetized Elementary Streams</b> (<i>PES</i>) are elementary streams <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Packet_(information_technology)" title="Packet (information technology)">packetized</a> into packets of variable lengths, i.e., divided ES into independent chunks where <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_redundancy_check" title="Cyclic redundancy check">cyclic redundancy check</a> (CRC) <a href="/wiki/Checksum" title="Checksum">checksum</a> was added to each packet for error detection.</p>
<p><b>System Clock Reference</b> (SCR) is a timing value stored in a 33-bit header of each PES, at a frequency/precision of 90 kHz, with an extra 9-bit extension that stores additional timing data with a precision of 27 MHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pack_header_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pack_header-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tutorial_stc_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-tutorial_stc-42">[42]</a></sup> These are inserted by the encoder, derived from the system time clock (STC). Simultaneously encoded audio and video streams will not have identical SCR values, however, due to buffering, encoding, jitter, and other delay.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_streams">Program streams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Program streams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/MPEG_program_stream" title="MPEG program stream">MPEG program stream</a>.</div>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/MPEG_program_stream" title="MPEG program stream">Program Streams</a></b> (PS) are concerned with combining multiple <b>packetized elementary streams</b> (usually just one audio and video PES) into a single stream, ensuring simultaneous delivery, and maintaining synchronization. The PS structure is known as a <a href="/wiki/Multiplexing" title="Multiplexing">multiplex</a>, or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Container_format_(digital)" title="Container format (digital)">container format</a>.</p>
<p><b>Presentation time stamps</b> (PTS) exist in PS to correct the inevitable disparity between audio and video SCR values (time-base correction). 90 kHz PTS values in the PS header tell the decoder which video SCR values match which audio SCR values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pack_header_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-pack_header-41">[41]</a></sup> PTS determines when to display a portion of an MPEG program, and is also used by the decoder to determine when data can be discarded from the <a href="/wiki/Data_buffer" title="Data buffer">buffer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tutorial_pts_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-tutorial_pts-43">[43]</a></sup> Either video or audio will be delayed by the decoder until the corresponding segment of the other arrives and can be decoded.</p>
<p>PTS handling can be problematic. Decoders must accept multiple <i>program streams</i> that have been concatenated (joined sequentially). This causes PTS values in the middle of the video to reset to zero, which then begin incrementing again. Such PTS wraparound disparities can cause timing issues that must be specially handled by the decoder.</p>
<p><b>Decoding Time Stamps</b> (DTS), additionally, are required because of B-frames. With B-frames in the video stream, adjacent frames have to be encoded and decoded out-of-order (re-ordered frames). DTS is quite similar to PTS, but instead of just handling sequential frames, it contains the proper time-stamps to tell the decoder when to decode and display the next B-frame (types of frames explained below), ahead of its anchor (P- or I-) frame. Without B-frames in the video, PTS and DTS values are identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tutorial_dts_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-tutorial_dts-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiplexing">Multiplexing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Multiplexing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To generate the PS, the multiplexer will interleave the (two or more) packetized elementary streams. This is done so the packets of the simultaneous streams can be transferred over the same <a href="/wiki/Channel_(communications)" title="Channel (communications)">channel</a> and are guaranteed to both arrive at the decoder at precisely the same time. This is a case of <a href="/wiki/Time-division_multiplexing" title="Time-division multiplexing">time-division multiplexing</a>.</p>
<p>Determining how much data from each stream should be in each interleaved segment (the size of the interleave) is complicated, yet an important requirement. Improper interleaving will result in buffer underflows or overflows, as the receiver gets more of one stream than it can store (e.g. audio), before it gets enough data to decode the other simultaneous stream (e.g. video). The MPEG <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_Buffering_Verifier" title="Video Buffering Verifier">Video Buffering Verifier</a> (VBV) assists in determining if a multiplexed PS can be decoded by a device with a specified data throughput rate and buffer size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tutorial_vbv_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-tutorial_vbv-45">[45]</a></sup> This offers feedback to the muxer and the encoder, so that they can change the mux size or adjust bitrates as needed for compliance.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_2:_Video">Part 2: Video</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Part 2: Video">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Part 2 of the MPEG-1 standard covers video and is defined in <b>ISO/IEC-11172-2</b>. The design was heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/H.261" title="H.261">H.261</a>.</p>
<p>MPEG-1 Video exploits perceptual compression methods to significantly reduce the data rate required by a video stream. It reduces or completely discards information in certain frequencies and areas of the picture that the human eye has limited ability to fully perceive. It also exploits temporal (over time) and spatial (across a picture) redundancy common in video to achieve better data compression than would be possible otherwise. (See: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_compression" title="Video compression">Video compression</a>)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_space">Color space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Color space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yuvformats420sampling.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="999" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yuvformats420sampling.svg/220px-Yuvformats420sampling.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yuvformats420sampling.svg/330px-Yuvformats420sampling.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Yuvformats420sampling.svg/440px-Yuvformats420sampling.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yuvformats420sampling.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Example of 4:2:0 subsampling. The two overlapping center circles represent chroma blue and chroma red (color) pixels, while the 4 outside circles represent the luma (brightness).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before encoding video to MPEG-1, the color-space is transformed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y%27CbCr" title="Y'CbCr">Y'CbCr</a> (Y'=Luma, Cb=Chroma Blue, Cr=Chroma Red). <a href="/wiki/Luma_(video)" title="Luma (video)">Luma</a> (brightness, resolution) is stored separately from <a href="/wiki/Chrominance" title="Chrominance">chroma</a> (color, hue, phase) and even further separated into red and blue components. The chroma is also subsampled to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4:2:0" title="4:2:0">4:2:0</a>, meaning it is reduced by one half vertically and one half horizontally, to just one quarter the resolution of the video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpeg_faqs1_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-mpeg_faqs1-1">[1]</a></sup> This software algorithm also has analogies in hardware, such as the output from a <a href="/wiki/Bayer_filter" title="Bayer filter">Bayer pattern filter</a>, common in digital colour cameras.</p>
<p>Because the human eye is much more sensitive to small changes in brightness (the Y component) than in color (the Cr and Cb components), <a href="/wiki/Chroma_subsampling" title="Chroma subsampling">chroma subsampling</a> is a very effective way to reduce the amount of video data that needs to be compressed. On videos with fine detail (high <a href="/wiki/Spatial_frequency#Visual_perception" title="Spatial frequency">spatial complexity</a>) this can manifest as chroma <a href="/wiki/Aliasing" title="Aliasing">aliasing</a> artifacts. Compared to other digital <a href="/wiki/Compression_artifact" title="Compression artifact">compression artifacts</a>, this issue seems to be very rarely a source of annoyance.</p>
<p>Because of subsampling, Y'CbCr video must always be stored using even dimensions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divisible" title="Divisible">divisible</a> by 2), otherwise chroma mismatch ("ghosts") will occur, and it will appear as if the color is ahead of, or behind the rest of the video, much like a shadow.</p>
<p>Y'CbCr is often inaccurately called <a href="/wiki/YUV" title="YUV">YUV</a> which is only used in the domain of <a href="/wiki/Analog_transmission" title="Analog transmission">analog</a> video signals. Similarly, the terms <a href="/wiki/Luminance" title="Luminance">luminance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrominance" title="Chrominance">chrominance</a> are often used instead of the (more accurate) terms luma and chroma.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resolution.2Fbitrate">Resolution/bitrate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Resolution/bitrate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>MPEG-1 supports resolutions up to 4095×4095 (12-bits), and bitrates up to 100 Mbit/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>MPEG-1 videos are most commonly seen using <a href="/wiki/Source_Input_Format" title="Source Input Format">Source Input Format</a> (SIF) resolution: 352x240, 352x288, or 320x240. These low resolutions, combined with a bitrate less than 1.5 Mbit/s, make up what is known as a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Constrained_parameters_bitstream&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Constrained parameters bitstream (page does not exist)">constrained parameters bitstream</a> (CPB), later renamed the "Low Level" (LL) profile in MPEG-2. This is the minimum video specifications any <a href="/wiki/Codec" title="Codec">decoder</a> should be able to handle, to be considered MPEG-1 <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/compliant" title="wikt:compliant">compliant</a>. This was selected to provide a good balance between quality and performance, allowing the use of reasonably inexpensive hardware of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frame.2Fpicture.2Fblock_types">Frame/picture/block types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Frame/picture/block types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>MPEG-1 has several frame/picture types that serve different purposes. The most important, yet simplest, is <b>I-frame</b>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="I-frames">I-frames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: I-frames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>I-frame</b> is an abbreviation for <b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/I-frame" title="wikt:I-frame">Intra-frame</a></b>, so-called because they can be decoded independently of any other frames. They may also be known as <b>I-pictures</b>, or <b>keyframes</b> due to their somewhat similar function to the <a href="/wiki/Key_frame" title="Key frame">key frames</a> used in animation. I-frames can be considered effectively identical to baseline <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a> images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>High-speed seeking through an MPEG-1 video is only possible to the nearest I-frame. When cutting a video it is not possible to start playback of a segment of video before the first I-frame in the segment (at least not without computationally intensive re-encoding). For this reason, I-frame-only MPEG videos are used in editing applications.</p>
<p>I-frame only compression is very fast, but produces very large file sizes: a factor of 3× (or more) larger than normally encoded MPEG-1 video, depending on how temporally complex a specific video is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup> I-frame only MPEG-1 video is very similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MJPEG" title="MJPEG">MJPEG</a> video. So much so that very high-speed and theoretically lossless (in reality, there are rounding errors) conversion can be made from one format to the other, provided a couple of restrictions (color space and quantization matrix) are followed in the creation of the bitstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_transcoding_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith_transcoding-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The length between I-frames is known as the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_pictures" title="Group of pictures">group of pictures</a> (GOP) size. MPEG-1 most commonly uses a GOP size of 15-18. i.e. 1 I-frame for every 14-17 non-I-frames (some combination of P- and B- frames). With more intelligent encoders, GOP size is dynamically chosen, up to some pre-selected maximum limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Limits are placed on the maximum number of frames between I-frames due to decoding complexing, decoder buffer size, recovery time after data errors, seeking ability, and accumulation of IDCT errors in low-precision implementations most common in hardware decoders (See: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">IEEE</a>-1180).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="P-frames">P-frames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: P-frames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>P-frame</b> is an abbreviation for <b>Predicted-frame</b>. They may also be called <b>forward-predicted frames</b>, or <b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inter-" title="wikt:inter-">inter-frames</a></b> (B-frames are also inter-frames).</p>
<p>P-frames exist to improve compression by exploiting the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/temporal" title="wikt:temporal">temporal</a> (over time) <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/redundancy" title="wikt:redundancy">redundancy</a> in a video. P-frames store only the <i>difference</i> in image from the frame (either an I-frame or P-frame) immediately preceding it (this reference frame is also called the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anchor" title="wikt:anchor">anchor</a> frame</i>).</p>
<p>The difference between a P-frame and its anchor frame is calculated using <i>motion vectors</i> on each <i>macroblock</i> of the frame (see below). Such motion vector data will be embedded in the P-frame for use by the decoder.</p>
<p>A P-frame can contain any number of intra-coded blocks, in addition to any forward-predicted blocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hp_transcoding_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-hp_transcoding-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>If a video drastically changes from one frame to the next (such as a <a href="/wiki/Cut_(transition)" title="Cut (transition)">cut</a>), it is more efficient to encode it as an I-frame.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="B-frames">B-frames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: B-frames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>B-frame</b> stands for <b>bidirectional-frame</b>. They may also be known as <b>backwards-predicted frames</b> or <b>B-pictures</b>. B-frames are quite similar to P-frames, except they can make predictions using both the previous and future frames (i.e. two anchor frames).</p>
<p>It is therefore necessary for the player to first decode the next I- or P- anchor frame sequentially after the B-frame, before the B-frame can be decoded and displayed. This means decoding B-frames requires larger <a href="/wiki/Data_buffer" title="Data buffer">data buffers</a> and causes an increased delay on both decoding and during encoding. This also necessitates the decoding time stamps (DTS) feature in the container/system stream (see above). As such, B-frames have long been subject of much controversy, they are often avoided in videos, and are sometimes not fully supported by hardware decoders.</p>
<p>No other frames are predicted from a B-frame. Because of this, a very low bitrate B-frame can be inserted, where needed, to help control the bitrate. If this was done with a P-frame, future P-frames would be predicted from it and would lower the quality of the entire sequence. However, similarly, the future P-frame must still encode all the changes between it and the previous I- or P- anchor frame. B-frames can also be beneficial in videos where the background behind an object is being revealed over several frames, or in fading transitions, such as scene changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>A B-frame can contain any number of intra-coded blocks and forward-predicted blocks, in addition to backwards-predicted, or bidirectionally predicted blocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bmrc_mpeg2_faq_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-bmrc_mpeg2_faq-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hp_transcoding_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-hp_transcoding-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="D-frames">D-frames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: D-frames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>MPEG-1 has a unique frame type not found in later video standards. <b>D-frames</b> or <b>DC-pictures</b> are independent images (intra-frames) that have been encoded using DC transform coefficients only (AC coefficients are removed when encoding D-frames—see DCT below) and hence are very low quality. D-frames are never referenced by I-, P- or B- frames. D-frames are only used for fast previews of video, for instance when seeking through a video at high speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given moderately higher-performance decoding equipment, fast preview can be accomplished by decoding I-frames instead of D-frames. This provides higher quality previews, since I-frames contain AC coefficients as well as DC coefficients. If the encoder can assume that rapid I-frame decoding capability is available in decoders, it can save bits by not sending D-frames (thus improving compression of the video content). For this reason, D-frames are seldom actually used in MPEG-1 video encoding, and the D-frame feature has not been included in any later video coding standards.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macroblocks">Macroblocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Macroblocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Macroblock" title="Macroblock">Macroblock</a></div>
<p>MPEG-1 operates on video in a series of 8x8 blocks for quantization. However, because chroma (color) is subsampled by a factor of 4, each pair of (red and blue) chroma blocks corresponds to 4 different luma blocks. This set of 6 blocks, with a resolution of 16x16, is called a <b>macroblock</b>.</p>
<p>A macroblock is the smallest independent unit of (color) video. Motion vectors (see below) operate solely at the macroblock level.</p>
<p>If the height or width of the video are not exact <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/multiple" title="wikt:multiple">multiples</a> of 16, full rows and full columns of macroblocks must still be encoded and decoded to fill out the picture (though the extra decoded pixels are not displayed).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_vectors">Motion vectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Motion vectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To decrease the amount of temporal redundancy in a video, only blocks that change are updated, (up to the maximum GOP size). This is known as <b>conditional replenishment</b>. However, this is not very effective by itself. Movement of the objects, and/or the camera may result in large portions of the frame needing to be updated, even though only the position of the previously encoded objects has changed. Through <b>motion estimation</b> the encoder can compensate for this movement and remove a large amount of redundant information.</p>
<p>The encoder compares the current frame with adjacent parts of the video from the anchor frame (previous I- or P- frame) in a diamond pattern, up to a (encoder-specific) predefined <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> limit from the area of the current macroblock. If a match is found, only the direction and distance (i.e. the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vector" title="wikt:vector"><i>vector</i></a> of the <i>motion</i>) from the previous video area to the current macroblock need to be encoded into the inter-frame (P- or B- frame). The reverse of this process, performed by the decoder to reconstruct the picture, is called <b><a href="/wiki/Motion_compensation" title="Motion compensation">motion compensation</a></b>.</p>
<p>A predicted macroblock rarely matches the current picture perfectly, however. The differences between the estimated matching area, and the real frame/macroblock is called the <b>prediction error</b>. The larger the error, the more data must be additionally encoded in the frame. For efficient video compression, it is very important that the encoder is capable of effectively and precisely performing motion estimation.</p>
<p>Motion vectors record the <i>distance</i> between two areas on screen based on the number of pixels (called <b>pels</b>). MPEG-1 video uses a motion vector (MV) precision of one half of one pixel, or <b>half-pel</b>. The finer the precision of the MVs, the more accurate the match is likely to be, and the more efficient the compression. There are trade-offs to higher precision, however. Finer MVs result in larger data size, as larger numbers must be stored in the frame for every single MV, increased coding complexity as increasing levels of interpolation on the macroblock are required for both the encoder and decoder, and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/law_of_diminishing_returns" title="wikt:law of diminishing returns">diminishing returns</a> (minimal gains) with higher precision MVs. Half-pel was chosen as the ideal trade-off. (See: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qpel" title="Qpel">qpel</a>)</p>
<p>Because neighboring macroblocks are likely to have very similar motion vectors, this redundant information can be compressed quite effectively by being stored <a href="/wiki/Pulse-code_modulation" title="Pulse-code modulation">DPCM</a>-encoded. Only the (smaller) amount of difference between the MVs for each macroblock needs to be stored in the final bitstream.</p>
<p>P-frames have one motion vector per macroblock, relative to the previous anchor frame. B-frames, however, can use two motion vectors; one from the previous anchor frame, and one from the future anchor frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hp_transcoding_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-hp_transcoding-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Partial macroblocks, and black borders/bars encoded into the video that do not fall exactly on a macroblock boundary, cause havoc with motion prediction. The block padding/border information prevents the macroblock from closely matching with any other area of the video, and so, significantly larger prediction error information must be encoded for every one of the several dozen partial macroblocks along the screen border. DCT encoding and quantization (see below) also isn't nearly as effective when there is large/sharp picture contrast in a block.</p>
<p>An even more serious problem exists with macroblocks that contain significant, random, <i>edge noise</i>, where the picture transitions to (typically) black. All the above problems also apply to edge noise. In addition, the added randomness is simply impossible to compress significantly. All of these effects will lower the quality (or increase the bitrate) of the video substantially.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DCT">DCT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: DCT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Each 8x8 block is encoded by first applying a <i>forward</i> <a href="/wiki/Discrete_cosine_transform" title="Discrete cosine transform">discrete cosine transform</a> (FDCT) and then a quantization process. The FDCT process (by itself) is theoretically lossless, and can be reversed by applying an <i>Inverse</i> DCT (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDCT" title="IDCT">IDCT</a>) to reproduce the original values (in the absence of any quantization and rounding errors). In reality, there are some (sometimes large) rounding errors introduced both by quantization in the encoder (as described in the next section) and by IDCT approximation error in the decoder. The minimum allowed accuracy of a decoder IDCT approximation is defined by ISO/IEC 23002-1. (Prior to 2006, it was specified by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=IEEE_1180&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="IEEE 1180 (page does not exist)">IEEE 1180</a>-1990.)</p>
<p>The FDCT process converts the 8x8 block of uncompressed pixel values (brightness or color difference values) into an 8x8 indexed array of <i>frequency coefficient</i> values. One of these is the (statistically high in variance) <b>DC coefficient</b>, which represents the average value of the entire 8x8 block. The other 63 coefficients are the statistically smaller <b>AC coefficients</b>, which are positive or negative values each representing sinusoidal deviations from the flat block value represented by the <i>DC coefficient</i>.</p>

</dl>
<p>Since the DC coefficient value is statistically correlated from one block to the next, it is compressed using <a href="/wiki/Pulse-code_modulation" title="Pulse-code modulation">DPCM</a> encoding. Only the (smaller) amount of difference between each DC value and the value of the DC coefficient in the block to its left needs to be represented in the final bitstream.</p>
<p>Additionally, the frequency conversion performed by applying the DCT provides a statistical decorrelation function to efficiently concentrate the signal into fewer high-amplitude values prior to applying quantization (see below).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantization">Quantization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=MPEG-1&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Quantization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quantization_(image_processing)" title="Quantization (image processing)">Quantization</a> (of digital data) is, essentially, the process of reducing the accuracy of a signal, by dividing it into some larger step size (i.e. finding the nearest multiple, and discarding the remainder/modulus).</p>
<p>The frame-level quantizer is a number from 0 to 31 (although encoders will usually omit/disable some of the extreme values) which determines how much information will be removed from a given frame. The frame-level quantizer is either dynamically selected by the encoder to maintain a certain user-specified bitrate, or (much less commonly) directly specified by the user.</p>
<p>Contrary to popular belief, a fixed frame-level quantizer (set by the user) does not deliver a constant level of quality. Instead, it is an arbitrary metric that will provide a somewhat varying level of quality, depending on the contents of each frame. Given two files of identical sizes, the one encoded at an average bitrate should look better than the one encoded with a fixed quantizer (variable bitrate). Constant quantizer encoding can be used, however, to accurately determine the minimum and maximum bitrates possible for encoding a given video.</p>
<p>A <b>quantization matrix</b> is a string of 64-numbers (0-255) which tells the encoder how relatively important or unimportant each piece of visual information is. Each number in the matrix corresponds to a certain frequency component of the video image.</p>
<p>An example quantization matrix:</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}16&amp;11&amp;10&amp;16&amp;24&amp;40&amp;51&amp;61\\12&amp;12&amp;14&amp;19&amp;26&amp;58&amp;60&amp;55\\14&amp;13&amp;16&amp;24&amp;40&amp;57&amp;69&amp;56\\14&amp;17&amp;22&amp;29&amp;51&amp;87&amp;80&amp;62\\18&amp;22&amp;37&amp;56&amp;68&amp;109&amp;103&amp;77\\24&amp;35&amp;55&amp;64&amp;81&amp;104&amp;113&amp;92\\49&amp;64&amp;78&amp;87&amp;103&amp;121&amp;120&amp;101\\72&amp;92&amp;95&amp;98&amp;112&amp;100&amp;103&amp;99\end{bmatrix}}}</annotation>

\begin{bmatrix}

 18 &amp; 22 &amp; 37 &amp; 56 &amp; 68 &amp; 109 &amp; 103 &amp; 77 \\
 24 &amp; 35 &amp; 55 &amp; 64 &amp; 81 &amp; 104 &amp; 113 &amp; 92 \\
 49 &amp; 64 &amp; 78 &amp; 87 &amp; 103 &amp; 121 &amp; 120 &amp; 101 \\
 72 &amp; 92 &amp; 95 &amp; 98 &amp; 112 &amp; 100 &amp; 103 &amp; 99
\end{bmatrix}
" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-math-element" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e3861b24e1e5cb44062d02190178320baffa5d7f" style="vertical-align: -12.171ex; width:43.589ex; height:25.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Quantization is performed by taking each of the 64 <i>frequency</i> values of the DCT block, dividing them by the frame-level quantizer, then dividing them by their corresponding values in the quantization matrix. Finally, the result is rounded down. This significantly reduces, or completely eliminates, the information in some frequency components of the picture. Typically, high frequency information is less visually important, and so high frequencies are much more <i>strongly quantized</i> (drastically reduced). MPEG-1 actually uses two separate quantization matrices, one for intra-blocks (I-blocks) and one for inter-block (P- and B- blocks) so quantization of different block types can be done independently, and so, more effectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Didier_MPEG_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Didier_MPEG-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>This quantization process usually reduces a significant number of the <i>AC coefficients</i> to zero, (known as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sparse" title="wikt:sparse">sparse</a> data) which can then be more efficiently compressed by entropy coding (lossless compression) in the next step.</p>
